Kettle Chips","summary":"Whether thick cut, crinkle, plain, or flavored, potato chips are one of America’s most beloved snack foods. Our favorite is the small-batch, kettle-style chip. These chips have good potato flavor and a crisp, light texture. Though seemingly simple, perfecting a homemade version proved to be a challenge. ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F2VFfH7njd9x80_KYwwvzCOFzyT4\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhether thick cut, crinkle, plain, or flavored, potato chips are one of America’s most beloved snack foods. Our favorite is the small-batch, kettle-style chip. These chips have good potato flavor and a crisp, light texture. Though seemingly simple, perfecting a homemade version proved to be a challenge. Frying at too low a temperature made them soggy and greasy, but if we increased the heat, they burned. Targeting the potatoes’ starch as the source of our troubles, we finally landed on a method of rinsing, parboiling, then frying. It was a bit of work, but the reward of fresh, golden homemade potato chips was well worth it. —\u003Cem\u003EAmerica’s Test Kitchen\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EFIND THIS RECIPE AND 100+ MORE LIKE IT IN OUR&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Famzn.to\u002F1eZzDOt\"\u003EDIY COOKBOOK\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMakes about 6 cups\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMake today, enjoy immediately\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1 pound medium Yukon Gold potatoes, sliced 1\u002F16 inch thick on mandoline\u003Cbr\u003E2 quarts vegetable oil\u003Cbr\u003EFine sea salt\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1. Line&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIm50\"\u003Erimmed baking sheet\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;with clean&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtI4Lm\"\u003Edish towel\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Set&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIfpS\"\u003Ewire rack\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;in second rimmed baking sheet and line with triple layer of paper towels; set both sheets aside. Place potato slices in large bowl and cover with cold water. Gently swirl potatoes to release starch. Drain potatoes and return to bowl. Repeat rinsing step until water no longer turns cloudy, about 5 rinses.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E2. Bring 2 quarts water to boil in&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIrFQ\"\u003Elarge saucepan\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;over high heat. Add potatoes, return to gentle boil, and cook until just beginning to soften, 2 to 3 minutes. Drain potatoes well, then spread out over dish towel–lined sheet and thoroughly pat dry.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E3. Meanwhile, heat oil in&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIxND\"\u003EDutch oven\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;over medium-high heat to 325 degrees. Carefully place one-quarter of potato slices in oil. Fry, stirring frequently with\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIFN2\"\u003Ewire skimmer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;or&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtIYYb\"\u003Eslotted spoon\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, until oil stops bubbling and chips turn golden and crisp, 3 to 4 minutes. Adjust burner, if necessary, to&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtJ8yV\"\u003Emaintain oil temperature\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;around 325 degrees. As soon as chips finish frying (some chips may cook slightly faster than others), transfer to prepared wire rack using wire skimmer or slotted spoon. Season with&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbit.ly\u002F1qtJgyh\"\u003Esalt\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;to taste.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E4. Return oil to 325 degrees and repeat with remaining potato slices in 3 more batches. Let cool completely before serving. (Kettle chips are best enjoyed the day they are made.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E","leadImages":[{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F2VFfH7njd9x80_KYwwvzCOFzyT4.GIF","type":"image\u002Fgif","width":500,"height":333,"size_type":"original"}],"lead_image":{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F2VFfH7njd9x80_KYwwvzCOFzyT4.GIF","type":"image\u002Fgif","width":500,"height":333,"size_type":"original"}},{"id":"79270582872","magazine":"food","priority":"normal","palette":["#80ced7","#f8c85a","#f8483e","#df9563","#7dcde1"],"author":{"id":"racheltepperyahoo","name":"Rachel Tepper","title":"Associate Food Editor","bio":"When she's not traveling to far-flung locales, Rachel lives in Brooklyn and enjoys searching for the perfect dumpling. ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FbRszppXUdtCFCfUsEdajTJymHWw\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E  \n\u003Ch3\u003ESmart By Design\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMeant to be used on top of the stove, a grill pan has ridges that are similar to the grates of a grill. These ridges give food enticing grill marks, plus they let fat drain. Cook anything in a grill pan (except large pieces like a leg of lamb!) that you would normally cook on the grill, such as chicken, vegetables, kebabs, and even fruit.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ERelated:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.marthastewart.com\u002F1047611\u002Fcomfort-fauxed-lighter-cures-complex-cravings?xsc=synd_yfood_ms\"\u003E14 New, Lighter Comfort Food Ideas\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ch3\u003EShopping Secrets\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EGrill pans are usually made from a nonstick-coated metal or from cast iron, which we prefer. They range in price from $20 to $50. We recommend cast iron — it conducts heat well and cooks foods evenly. To “grill” for a crowd, choose a pan that fits across two burners. Before using a cast-iron pan, season it according to manufacturers’ instructions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ERelated:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.marthastewart.com\u002F275548\u002F15-minutes-or-less-dessert-recipes\u002F@center\u002F276948\u002Fdinner-tonight?xsc=synd_yfood_ms\"\u003EDelicious Desserts in 15 Minutes or Less\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ch3\u003EPrepping and Cleaning\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPreheat the grill pan over medium heat for about 2 minutes or until very hot. Oil the pan before placing the food on it. After cooking, rinse the pan in warm, soapy water, loosening cooked-on bits with a stiff brush, and then dry thoroughly to prevent rusting.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMore Coffee Goes Modern","summary":"The classic Irish coffee was invented in the 1940s on a cold winter’s night in Ireland’s Shannon Airport and brought to the U.S. by Stanton Delaplane, a travel writer for the San Francisco Chronicle, who worked with San Francisco’s Buena Vista Cafe to popularize the drink in the 1950s.  Each of these 6 recipes injects a new, precise twist into the ingredients or presentation, but without changing the fundamentals that have made it a classic. ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EJessica Colley\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EEvery bar has coffee, whiskey, and cream—and combining them with a touch of brown sugar results in a mighty fine cocktail. The classic Irish coffee was invented in the 1940s on a cold winter’s night in Ireland’s Shannon Airport and brought to the U.S. by&nbsp;Stanton Delaplane,&nbsp;a travel writer for the&nbsp;\u003Cem\u003ESan Francisco Chronicle,&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fem\u003Ewho&nbsp;worked with San Francisco’s Buena Vista Cafe to popularize the drink in the 1950s.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ENow, 60-ish years later, the quintessential winter warmer is going through another evolution, thanks to innovative bartenders who&nbsp;are infusing whiskey with ground espresso, whipping a stout reduction into fresh cream, and even heating a sous-vide bath to 75.2 degrees to keep the coffee\u002Fsugar mixture at the perfect temperature. Each of these 6 recipes injects a new, precise twist into the ingredients or presentation, but without changing the fundamentals that have made it a classic.&nbsp;As&nbsp;Sam Treadway, bartender at&nbsp;Backbar&nbsp;in Somerville, Massachusetts, puts it, the drink sticks around not only because it’s delicious but because it satisfies the “upper and downer effect we all need sometimes.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E“Plus,” he adds, “it’s a way classier choice than a vodka–Red Bull.”\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FapARwQKPiU6y0vsItmUq-tC0-zQ\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Fblind-abbot?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThe Blind Abbot:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E Coffee can be the fussiest ingredient in an Irish coffee. To avoid flavor degradation, dilution, or browning of coffee on a hot plate, Pamela Wiznitzer, who contributed to the cocktail list at Grace, a pub in New York City, uses cold-brew coffee—amped up with Galliano Ristretto, an espresso-based liqueur.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FQBUXyCEKAg-xhJqakQYuqw2EHLc\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Fvintage-coffee-cocktail?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBenicea Boy Irish Coffee\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E: “You don’t need to muck around with beans from the Himalayas,” says Belfast native Jack McGarry, owner-bartender of the Dead Rabbit Grocery and Grog in New York City. Yet there are a few common coffee mistakes that McGarry avoids when assembling his Irish coffee. “Espresso or Americano coffee overpowers the smooth and mellow Irish whiskey,” McGarry says, so he opts for filter coffee instead. And rather than leaving the coffee on the back bar for hours—i.e., ruining it—McGarry uses a sous-vide water bath to regulate the temperature of the coffee-sugar mixture. For whiskey, McGarry chooses a blend like Tullamore Dew or Jameson Original. “The grain content in the blend adds sweetness and kind of binds all the ingredients,” he adds. McGarry pre-whips cream by hand and stores it in a pourable container in the fridge. With all three ingredients ready to go, his bar staff can knock out the Irish coffee orders at a dangerously efficient pace.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FBcTAD_MJKBCPmlrt8NDss3-Kb5w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Fpour-irish-coffee?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPour Over Irish Coffee:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E At Backbar in Boston, bartender Joe Cammarata wanted to create a memorable experience, so his Irish coffee is fairly theatrical. In front of the customer, he places a pour-over-coffee setup atop a preheated glass. “We add whiskey and boiling water into the pour-over and let it strain,” he says. “Then we whip up heavy cream in a shaker tin and dollop it on top.” He finishes the drink with a few drops of Bittermans Xocolat Mole bitters.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FA7PgpqZPiRHGWpobnj6vPqEB28s\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Fvintage-coffee-cocktail?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003EVintage Coffee Cocktail\u003C\u002Fa\u003E:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Gareth Lambe, general manager of Vintage Cocktail Club in Dublin, Ireland, gives his coffee-whiskey cocktail another Irish twist: He reduces Guinness with malt extract and vanilla bean, then whips it into the cream, which gives it a “slightly malty taste with a back note of vanilla and stout.” This cream is floated on top of the coffee-whiskey mixture, providing a slightly sweet note that, he says, “marries perfectly with the bitterness of the coffee we use.”\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FCXesNyY8K1bE9pqibxFQVqTq6vM\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Firish-iced-coffee?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIrish Iced Coffee:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E At Restaurant Orsay in Jacksonville, Florida, bartenders infuse Tullamore Dew Irish whiskey with ground espresso—“to add depth of flavor and an extra jolt,” says bar manager Alex Smith. He then combines the infusion with 24-hour cold-brew coffee (from a Japanese system) and a touch of demerara syrup. He shakes it, and serves it over ice in a Collins glass; cream is then floated tableside.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipe\u002Fgorts-reprieve-irish-coffee?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FEC5MadltPIdFrIAhVZw1_oRMsFg\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGort’s Reprieve Irish Coffee:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Drink.well. in Austin, sees the coffee as the chance for a bit of theater. “We brew a fresh French press every time someone orders the drink,” says bartender Jessica Sanders. “A French press yields a coffee with a bit more viscosity and bolder flavors, compared to a traditional drip brewer.” The house coffee is from Casa Brasil, a local roaster focused exclusively on Brazilian beans. “Brazilian coffees,” says Sanders, “have the perfect level of acidity—lots of lime zest and a great astringency that balances out what can be a heavy drink with the whipped cream.” But, she says, simply using Irish whiskey is a solid foundation. “So long as you are using an actual Irish whiskey and not reaching for Bailey’s or another ‘Irish cream,’ you’re off to a good start.”\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGORT’S REPRIEVE IRISH COFFEE\u003Cbr\u003EIngredients:&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E4 ounces hot freshly brewed coffee \u003Cbr\u003E1&nbsp;1\u002F2 ounces Redbreast Irish Whiskey \u003Cbr\u003E3\u002F4 ounce amaro (such as Luxardo Amaro Abano) \u003Cbr\u003E1 teaspoon simple syrup \u003Cbr\u003E4 dashes Bittermens New Orleans Coffee Bitters (optional) \u003Cbr\u003EWhipped cream, ground nutmeg, cinnamon, and allspice (for serving) \u003Cbr\u003E1 fresh mint leaf 2 coffee beans\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPreparation:\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EStir coffee, whiskey, liqueur, simple syrup, and bitters in a glass to combine. Top with whipped cream, nutmeg, cinnamon, and allspice and garnish with mint leaf and coffee beans.\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg Vinegar Pie","summary":"At Comfort, in Richmond, Virginia, chef de cuisine Travis Milton is bringing back a delicious tradition with vinegars made from locally grown ingredients such as turnip greens, tomatoes, and honeydew melon.  Milton glazes wings with a barbecue sauce made from Mountain Dew vinegar, and bakes homemade Cheerwine vinegar into a pie that harks back to the days when Appalachian cooks, lacking citrus, substituted a more common ingredient with a similar bite.  Photographs by Margaret Houston Milton’s vinegar pie, which he tops with a dollop of corncob jelly cream, is a popular dessert at Comfort.  His recipe for Cheerwine vinegar makes enough for a season’s worth of pies—with plenty left over for other projects.","body":"\u003Cp\u003EBy the editors of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fgardenandgun.com\u002F?utm_source=yahoo.com&amp;utm_medium=yahoo&amp;utm_campaign=gardenandgunhomepagevinegarpie&amp;utm_content=gardenandgunhomepage\" title=\"Garden and Gun\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003EGarden &amp; Gun\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EHere’s something you may not know: You can make vinegar from just about any sugary liquid. Sweet tea, fruit juice, even soda—with time and a little bit of effort, they’ll all transform. And before supermarkets brought Heinz and other big brands to the masses, people across the country brewed vinegar at home.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAt \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.comfortrva.com\u002F\" title=\"Comfort\"\u003EComfort\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, in Richmond, Virginia, chef de cuisine Travis Milton is bringing back a delicious tradition with vinegars made from locally grown ingredients such as turnip greens, tomatoes, and honeydew melon. He isn’t just drawing from the garden, either. Milton glazes wings with a barbecue sauce made from Mountain Dew vinegar, and bakes homemade \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fgardenandgun.com\u002Farticle\u002Fmaking-cheerwine-vinegar\u002F?utm_source=yahoo.com&amp;utm_medium=yahoo&amp;utm_campaign=cheerwinevinegar&amp;utm_content=cheerwinevinegar\" title=\"Cheerwine vinegar\"\u003ECheerwine vinegar\u003C\u002Fa\u003E into a pie that harks back to the days when Appalachian cooks, lacking citrus, substituted a more common ingredient with a similar bite.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FG8F56lVFARLu3H2OPVwzzS6qDZg\"\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhotographs by Margaret Houston\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMilton’s vinegar pie, which he tops with a dollop of corncob jelly cream, is a popular dessert at Comfort. His recipe for \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fgardenandgun.com\u002Farticle\u002Fmaking-cheerwine-vinegar\u002F?utm_source=yahoo.com&amp;utm_medium=yahoo&amp;utm_campaign=cheerwinevinegar&amp;utm_content=cheerwinevinegar\" title=\"Cheerwine vinegar\"\u003ECheerwine vinegar\u003C\u002Fa\u003E makes enough for a season’s worth of pies—with plenty left over for other projects. And if you’re not a fan of Cheerwine, there’s no reason why you can’t use a different soda. Mountain Dew vinaigrette, anyone?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FttjFz3nxKs1fRF68d8sDcCQKKmk\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECheerwine&nbsp;Vinegar Pie\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cem\u003EFrom Travis Milton, Comfort, Richmond, Virginia\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPie Crust\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E2 cups all-purpose flour\u003Cbr\u003E1\u002F8&nbsp;tsp. salt\u003Cbr\u003E1 tbsp. sugar\u003Cbr\u003E&frac12; cup cornmeal*\u003Cbr\u003E2 tbsp. lard\u003Cbr\u003E&frac12; cup butter, cut into small pieces\u003Cbr\u003E&frac12; cup chilled buttermilk\u003Cbr\u003E1 tsp. apple cider vinegar\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ECombine flour, salt, sugar, and cornmeal together, and sift into a large mixing bowl. Add lard and work it into the dry ingredients with your fingers until it is well incorporated. Repeat with butter, and then transfer the contents of the bowl to a food processor.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPulse the ingredients 3-4 times, or until the mixture resembles a coarse meal. Return the mixture to the bowl, add buttermilk and cider vinegar, and stir until it begins to form a dough. Then knead the dough into a coherent shape, folding it over itself in order to give the crust a layered, flaky texture.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ETransfer the dough to a floured surface, and roll it into a circle 12-13 inches in diameter and about 1\u002F8-inch thick. Lightly flour the top of the crust and carefully roll it onto the rolling pin. Unroll it over a 9-inch pie pan, crimp the edges, and dot the base with a knife 8-10 times. Refrigerate until ready to use.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPie Filling\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E3 tbsp. cornmeal*\u003Cbr\u003E1\u002F8 tsp. salt\u003Cbr\u003E1&frac34; cup sugar\u003Cbr\u003E3 whole eggs and 2 yolks, beaten together\u003Cbr\u003E&frac14; cup buttermilk\u003Cbr\u003E&frac14; cup \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fgardenandgun.com\u002Farticle\u002Fmaking-cheerwine-vinegar\u002F?utm_source=yahoo.com&amp;utm_medium=yahoo&amp;utm_campaign=cheerwinevinegar&amp;utm_content=cheerwinevinegar\" title=\"Cheerwine vinegar\"\u003ECheerwine vinegar\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E1\u002F3 cup melted unsalted butter\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPreheat the oven to 350 degrees.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ESift cornmeal, salt, and sugar together into a large mixing bowl. Then gently whisk in eggs. Once the eggs are thoroughly incorporated, add buttermilk, vinegar, and butter.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPour custard mixture into an unbaked pie crust and bake for 40-45 minutes, or until a medium-brown crust has formed on top and the pie has slightly less jiggle than Jello. Let cool and enjoy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E*Milton uses coarse sorghum flour instead of cornmeal. If you can find it, it adds extra flavor to both the crust and the filling.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E","leadImages":[{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w175\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":175,"height":219,"size_type":"175"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs3.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w200\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":200,"height":250,"size_type":"200"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w250\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":250,"height":313,"size_type":"250"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w300\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":300,"height":375,"size_type":"300"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w350\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":350,"height":438,"size_type":"350"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w400\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":400,"height":500,"size_type":"400"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w450\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":450,"height":563,"size_type":"450"},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":625,"size_type":"original"}],"lead_image":{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs2.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F7583ca25d73cc90b50676577f589770ff1fbb7e7","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":625,"size_type":"original"}},{"id":"79099518796","magazine":"food","priority":"normal","palette":["#d79a6c","#3a6d9e","#82492f","#609acd","#304a64"],"author":{" The aprons his servers wear were hand-sewn to his specifications. Qui commissioned the mural on the wall, which was painted by a Japanese artist he likes. And he convinced architecture firm \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Faparallel.com\u002F\"\u003EA Parallel\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to build the space, even though A Parallel generally sticks to residential projects, because their work so perfectly matched his vision.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhat he made sure the architects included in their blueprint: an open kitchen, so Qui can see his guests. “I like watching people eat,” he says. “Maybe that sounds weird, but I encourage my \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftagged\u002Fchefs\"\u003Echefs\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to do that, too.” Based on his diners’ reactions, says Qui, he might change a dish. “\u003Cem\u003EHow\u003C\u002Fem\u003E they eat a dish is very important. That will tell me what nuances in the dish should go away or don’t matter.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EQui received this hawk eye training from working at a sushi bar. “Sushi chefs are always looking at what people are eating—and what they’re not eating,” he says. “The proof is on the plate. Most people won’t \u003Cem\u003Etell\u003C\u002Fem\u003E you what is bad even when you ask them, but you can \u003Cem\u003Esee\u003C\u002Fem\u003E what they’re leaving there.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAll that watching and learning plus a pretty hefty portfolio of drawing and sculpture have made Qui a master of artfully serving food. Who better to learn some tricks from, then? Herewith, Paul Qui’s Guide to Plating:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FBsdys3M8wMJOusicpsCUQoVMB3w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThere’s beauty in blankness.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;”Negative space always enhances the plate and enhances the perspective of the food.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENix the stacks.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;”I hate it when people make things super tall. If [the dish] needs a little height, you give it a little height, but you don’t want a tower.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002Fs0h1Zp0OsEh2m60NCf0fwBOmsG0\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPut imperfection on a pedestal.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;”I like things to look organic. When you go to \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Felevenmadisonpark.com\u002F\"\u003EEMP\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, for example, you have these punched little circles of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fgenius-miso-creamed-kale-70605701742.html\"\u003Ekale\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and a cube of something and it’s all perfect. There are some plates I do appreciate that way, but generally I like the natural lines of what the product is, whether it’s a slice of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ffennel-the-best-thing-to-buy-best-way-to-eat-it-now-77404590636.html\"\u003Efennel\u003C\u002Fa\u003E or a green or whatever.” Similarly, “nothing is going to be symmetrical all in a row.” This applies to composed bites, like sushi, but also to simple dishes like a steak. “\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftreat-me-right-says-steak-69802015485.html\"\u003ESteak\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, for me, is all it needs to be, when it’s cooked beautifully. So just put it on a plate—but put it slightly off-center.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThe spoon is your friend.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;”You get a sense of this when you work the line a lot, but there’s a certain finesse in kind of \u003Cem\u003Edraping\u003C\u002Fem\u003E things with the spoon. The trick is to focus on \u003Cem\u003Ehow\u003C\u002Fem\u003E you’re putting it on the plate: do you use the tip of the spoon or the side?” So play around. “You’re trying to make nicer-looking piles of food, basically.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FY2FPuBfhxaZSXU1-sLy4AKHQAic\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ETry ‘The Curling Iron’.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E For \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fits-a-taxi-that-serves-noodles-made-just-for-72110952383.html\"\u003Enoodle\u003C\u002Fa\u003E dishes, “use chopsticks kind of like a curling iron”: dress the noodles \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fin-a-bowl-how-basically-everything-should-be-eaten-77076205181.html\"\u003Ein a bowl\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by swirling the chopsticks in their center, then transfer the noodles onto the plate that way, set them down, and gently remove the sticks. Then add more sauce and whatever garnishes you might have on top of the noodles. “It will look sexier,” says Qui.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAdd a pop of color.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;”Take beef bourguignon, for example. I save a little bit of the veg that goes into it (carrot, onion), blanche them separately in some stock, and then place them on the top of each bowl of beef bourguignon. The \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fcomfort-foods-that-arent-brown-75604326776.html\"\u003Ecolor\u003C\u002Fa\u003E comes from those bright vegetables that aren’t super cooked-down [like the rest of the dish].”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FPJEF7hOSX4S5kE5jEwzfTJpPxwY\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: All, Bonjwing Lee\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYahoo Food is a new site for people who love to eat. Computer at Helm of Food Truck","summary":"It’s not every day you get to eat bread pudding made by a computer.  ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FS2gRxWYM4ITg5D3y8jXfg3oc7OY\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Falexvanburen\"\u003EAlex Van Buren\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.imdb.com\u002Ftitle\u002Ftt1798709\u002F\"\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIt’s not every day you get to eat \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fcook-this-tonight-savory-bread-pudding-75605622678.html\"\u003Ebread pudding\u003C\u002Fa\u003E made by a computer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EOne of the most buzzed-about food options at \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fsxsw.com\u002Fhttps:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftagged\u002Fsxsw\"\u003ESXSW\u003C\u002Fa\u003E—where it’s tough to make noise that can be heard above the constant hum of excitement—is the food truck run by&nbsp;Watson, “Jeopardy!” champion and inventor of the Austrian Chocolate Beef Burrito.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EOne of Watson’s newest projects is called “\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ibm.com\u002Fsmarterplanet\u002Fus\u002Fen\u002Fcognitivecooking\u002Ffood.html?cmp=usbrb&amp;cm=s&amp;csr=cognitive_cooking_site&amp;cr=home&amp;ct=usbrb301\"\u003Ecognitive cooking\u003C\u002Fa\u003E,” which is sort of a variation on “\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.imdb.com\u002Ftitle\u002Ftt1798709\u002F\"\u003EHer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&quot; (if Scarlett Johansson was a disembodied chef, not a disembodied romantic interest).&nbsp;Using an iPad, you&nbsp;give Watson a starting ingredient, such as saffron. The computer then prompts you to pick a cuisine in which saffron is sometimes found, whether it’s common, like Indian cuisine, or less common, such as Azerbaijani cuisine.&nbsp;Finally, the iPad suggests a few dishes, such as a cake, that might include the ingredient and the cuisine. It suggests general directions on how to make a cake, and then suggests additional ingredients, such as cherries and plums. (\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.engadget.com\u002F2014\u002F03\u002F08\u002Fwatson-food-truck\u002F?ncid=rss_truncated\"\u003EEngadget did a great job investigating how this might work\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut the additional ingredients the computer suggests are a little outr&eacute;, since it goes a step further, analyzing all the ingredients in the world on a molecular level to realize that hey, mushrooms and strawberries are not that different, so why not put them together? The result are dishes like&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ibm.com\u002Fsmarterplanet\u002Fus\u002Fen\u002Fcognitivecooking\u002Ffood.html?cmp=usbrb&amp;cm=s&amp;csr=cognitive_cooking_site&amp;cr=home&amp;ct=usbrb301\"\u003EVietnamese Granny Smith Apple Kebab\u003C\u002Fa\u003E,&nbsp;”Ceviche Fish and Chips,” and bacon bread pudding with cumin (which we sampled today).&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ESince computers can’t yet cook, humans help bring this technology to life, and they’re no slouches in the cooking department:&nbsp;”Chopped” champion \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fjamesbriscione.com\u002Fwordpress\u002F\"\u003EJames Briscione\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;and&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Fmlaiskonis\"\u003EMichael&nbsp;Laiskonis\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, former Le Bernardin pastry wizard, are affiliated with the Institute for Culinary Education. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Fmlaiskonis\"\u003ELaiskonis\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;told us his favorite dish to date was yesterday’s \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ibm.com\u002Fsmarterplanet\u002Fus\u002Fen\u002Fcognitivecooking\u002Ffood.html?cmp=usbrb&amp;cm=s&amp;csr=cognitive_cooking_site&amp;cr=home&amp;ct=usbrb301\"\u003Ebeef burrito with chocolate,\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;which also included edamame and vanilla bean. (It makes sense that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Flayers-of-clothing-layers-of-blankets-when-the-66993562247.html\"\u003Echocolate could work with savory ingredients\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.) But today marked his first time ever incorporating cumin into a dessert (this from a \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ibm.com\u002Fsmarterplanet\u002Fus\u002Fen\u002Fcognitivecooking\u002Ffood.html?cmp=usbrb&amp;cm=s&amp;csr=cognitive_cooking_site&amp;cr=home&amp;ct=usbrb301\"\u003EJames Beard Award winner\u003C\u002Fa\u003E!) We thought the dish, which included a few cubes of savory bread, a pudding-like base, and figs, was good, not great, and we barely tasted the cumin… but it was also our first meal of the day after a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002F12-signs-of-a-truly-great-dive-bar-79178937416.html\"\u003Edaunting bar crawl\u003C\u002Fa\u003E the prior evening, so our taste buds may not have been set to top-quality tasting capability. Signs of a Truly Great Dive Bar","summary":"We attempted to answer the question of what makes a great dive bar Sunday night by completing a bar crawl of Austin’s best dives.  The destination list was compiled based on recommendations from Franklin Barbecue’s Aaron Franklin, Ned Elliott of Foreign and Domestic, bartender Justin Elliott of Qui, Christopher Jacobs from Jeffrey’s restaurant, and you, our dear readers.","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F4CTP_4gcZtRKR7U49fQsXWf17Hs\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: Julia Bainbridge\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe attempted to answer the question of what makes a truly great dive bar Sunday night by completing a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftagged\u002Fbars\"\u003Ebar\u003C\u002Fa\u003E crawl of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftagged\u002Fsxsw\"\u003EAustin’s\u003C\u002Fa\u003E best dives.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe destination list was compiled based on recommendations from \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ffranklin-bbq-by-the-numbers-78973377166.html\"\u003EFranklin Barbecue’s Aaron Franklin\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, Ned Elliott of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ffndaustin.com\u002F\"\u003EForeign and Domestic\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, bartender Justin Elliott of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fquiaustin.com\u002F\"\u003EQui\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fhow-to-make-a-martini-like-a-guy-whose-job-is-to-work-a-78807451531.html\"\u003EChristopher Jacobs from Jeffrey’s restaurant\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and you, our dear readers. It contained: Buddy’s Place, Lala’s, the Broken Spoke, Poodle Dog Lounge, Ginny’s Little Longhorn Saloon, Deep Eddy Cabaret, Dirty Bill’s, and G&amp;S Lounge. (Note: This was whittled down based on overlapping recommendations; there are many more great dives in Austin than these eight.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ENot all of them were open (Sunday is a day of rest in these parts), but most were—enough to get a taste of the good stuff. We mapped our course, we put our feet on the gas, and we launched ourselves into the night. These are the qualities that, throughout the trip, struck us as essential to a great dive:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E* When you approach, unsure of whether or not it’s actually open.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When you tell the bartender you were unsure of whether or not it was open and she says, “Oh s*&amp;t!” and runs into the back to flip on the lit sign.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When you ask for whiskey and you’re told: “No,”&nbsp;blank stare and following silence included. Also known as: When bartenders are nothing but sassy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When only beer and Doritos are on offer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When most men present are wearing steel-toed boots.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When a game of pool is 75 cents.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When a foot-wide rectangle has been cut in the bottom of the bathroom door so you can see whether or not there are feet beyond the threshold, indicating that it’s in use.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When sad, sad country music is the only thing on the juke box.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When there’s a juke box.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When the hand-written sign above the register reads, “No fussin’, no cussin’, no hasselin’, no wresslin’.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When, by the end of the night, you and everyone else in the bar are in one big happy crap-talking conversation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E*&nbsp;When you finally leave after a round of hugs.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F1PV6FaopB0Sq_3dl_RdJAJuPIxg\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: Julia Bainbridge\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EOf course, this list is particular to Austin. There would be no steel-toed boots in a great New Jersey dive. Patsy Cline’s velvet voice might not be flowing through a San Francisco joint.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut what’s great about dive bars is that they’re cheap, they’re consistent, and they’ve got no frills and no pressure—but damn if they don’t have style (often charmingly leftover from 1970).&nbsp;They’re not trying to be anything other than a place to come sit for a while. No one in front of or behind the bar cares what your job is or where you went to college. Hell, they don’t care who you are at all. And that’s why you go there: to be comfortable; to be anonymous; to celebrate with a couple friends; to silently drown a little heartache in a warm, flat beer. To do whatever you want.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYahoo Food is a new site for people who love to eat. It’s been a rough couple of weeks. Here are the results.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIce Water:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Surprise! This doesn’t work. My mouth felt bad. Thai bird chiles are spicy. I mean, I guess the cold numbed the pain somewhat while it was in my mouth. But as soon as I swallowed, the stupid-hot fury of the chile overcame me once again. What have I gotten myself into??\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE:&nbsp;\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipes\u002Fslideshow\u002F25-ways-to-use-sriracha?slide=1?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E25 Ways to Use Sriracha\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhole Milk:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E OK, this is kind of a personal thing, but I think drinking milk is disgusting. Like, I’ve probably had one glass of milk in my entire life. So, while chugging a glass of cold cow juice did mitigate the heat in my mouth considerably, I think I would have rather eaten two chiles, suffered doubly, and called it a day, thanks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EYogurt:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E What a relief to finally feel some, well, relief! The yogurt coated my mouth like a rich, fermented, dairy-based fire blanket, soothing my scorched palate more and more with each creamy bite. Fantastic.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBeer:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E A cold can of suds is usually a given when I’m eating spicy food, but does it really do anything to extinguish a burning tongue? It did…sort of. The coldness helped to dull the pain, and the lively carbonation distracted me from it, but any relief I experienced was temporary. All the more reason to crack another!\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE:&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Fcolumns\u002Fguinea-pig\u002Fslideshow\u002Fwhat-not-to-eat-with-sriracha\u002F?slide=1?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003EWhat Not to Eat with Sriracha\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EVodka:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E Some scientificious-sounding online source suggested that alcohol helps to dissolve capsaicin, so I figured I’d give a glass of cold vodka a shot. It worked surprisingly well—the pain got duller and diffused more with every sip I took—but, as expected, it also made me feel really drunk. Seems like a good choice in case of a chile-related emergency, but swilling Grey Goose between bites of five-alarm chili doesn’t sound that sustainable to me.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EOlive Oil Mouth Rinse:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E You know what’s worse than having your mouth completely inflamed by merciless chile heat? Having your mouth completely inflamed by merciless chile heat AND filled with olive oil. I don’t know who suggested this one, but I hate them. This totally sucked, and did not even kind of work. Boo.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWINNER: White Rice:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E This was the real sleeper hit—two big mouthfuls of cold leftover rice banished the pain from my mouth for good. I’m no scientist, but I’m thinking that the abrasive quality of the grains helped to dislodge the capsaicin from my flaming taste buds in a way that the liquids I tried just couldn’t. Awesome.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESee more from Bon Appetit:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipes\u002Fslideshow\u002F25-ways-to-use-sriracha?slide=1?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Ca Naked\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Ftest-kitchen\u002Fcommon-mistakes\u002Farticle\u002Fhow-to-ruin-popcorn?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E5 Ways to Makes Sculptures from Forks, Knives, and Spoons","summary":"All photos: Gary Hovey For 25 years, Gary Hovey labored as a heavy equipment operator and industrial welder, but his true passion was a touch more whimsical.  A decade ago, the Knoxville, Ohio resident was inspired by a 10-foot-tall horse sculpture made of steel bumpers by Chicago artist John Kearney.  Hovey resolved to make a few smaller sculptures of his own, but with materials he had on hand.","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FnKRkGgkNeKzrtvWycJfnQ0fUw6E\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EAll photos: Gary Hovey\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EFor 25 years, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.hoveyware.com\u002F\"\u003EGary Hovey\u003C\u002Fa\u003E labored as a heavy equipment operator and industrial welder, but his true passion was a touch more whimsical.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EA decade ago, the Knoxville, Ohio resident was inspired by a \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.johnkearneysculptor.com\u002F#steel-bumper-work\u002F5\"\u003E10-foot-tall horse sculpture \u003C\u002Fa\u003Emade of steel bumpers by&nbsp;Chicago artist John Kearney. Hovey resolved to make a few smaller sculptures of his own, but with materials he had on hand.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&quot;I thought: ‘I could make those with spoons,’&quot; Hovey told us. &quot;They’d be smaller, and people could have them in their homes.&quot; And that’s exactly what he did, first welding a dog and then a heron. They were quickly purchased, and Hovey realized he’d struck upon something.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&quot;People liked them,&quot; Hovey said.&nbsp;&quot;[The sculptures] make people happy.&quot; Many customers don’t realize at first how the sculptures are constructed, which Hovey takes delight in. &quot;It is funny to watch people as it dawns on their faces: ‘It’s flatware!’&quot;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EHovey’s sculptures, which are made exclusively with forks, knives and spoons, range in size from teeny tiny (about 2 inches tall) to enormous (his largest, a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002Fphoto.php?fbid=203256123021498&amp;set=pb.203244449689332.-2207520000.1394472488.&amp;type=3&amp;theater\"\u003Egiraffe\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, clocks in at just about six feet tall). They’re priced accordingly, between&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.buzzfeed.com\u002Fkeelyflaherty\u002Fthese-animal-sculptures-made-entirely-out-of-cutlery-will-am\"\u003E$50 to $5,000\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. To date, Hovey said he’s sculpted more than 100 pieces.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut for Hovey, the sculptures are more than just art; they’re healing. Diagnosed with \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fhealth.yahoo.net\u002Fcaring\u002F10-signs-of-parkinson-s-disease\"\u003EParkinson’s disease\u003C\u002Fa\u003E about ten years ago, Hovey was forced to give up his day job halfway through his illness. Today, he can’t drive or carry heavy objects, but he can still sculpt.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&quot;That I can still do it is amazing to me, even,&quot; Hovey mused. &quot;It’s therapy for me. Corned Beef and Cabbage","summary":"To prevent overcooking and overflowing, your slow cooker should be at least halfway but no more than two-thirds full when you start cooking. Need even more time than a recipe allows? Chilling the crock and ingredients buys 1 to 1 1\u002F2 more hours of cooking time.Don’t peek (too often), every time you open the lid it increases cooking time by about 15 minutes.  ","body":"\u003Cp\u003ETo prevent overcooking and overflowing, your slow cooker should be at least halfway but no more than two-thirds full when you start cooking. Need even more time than a recipe allows? Chilling the crock and ingredients buys 1 to 1&nbsp;1\u002F2 more hours of cooking time.Don’t peek (too often), every time you open the lid it increases cooking time by about 15 minutes.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E2 celery stalks, cut into 3-inch pieces\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E3 carrots, cut into 3-inch pieces\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1 small yellow onion, cut into 1-inch wedges (root end left intact)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1\u002F2 pound small potatoes, halved if large\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E6 sprigs thyme\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1 corned beef brisket (about 3 pounds), plus pickling spice packet or 1 tablespoon pickling spice\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E1\u002F2 head Savoy cabbage, cut into 1&nbsp;1\u002F2-inch wedges\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EGrainy mustard, for serving\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E1.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E In a 5-to-6-quart slow cooker, place celery, carrots, onion, potatoes, and thyme. Place corned beef, fat side up, on top of vegetables and sprinkle with pickling spice; add enough water to almost cover meat (4 to 6 cups). Cover and cook on high until corned beef is tender, 4&nbsp;1\u002F4 hours (or 8&nbsp;1\u002F2 hours on low). Arrange cabbage over corned beef, cover, and continue cooking until cabbage is tender, 45 minutes (or 1&nbsp;1\u002F2 hours on low). Thinly slice corned beef against the grain and serve with vegetables, cooking liquid, and grainy mustard.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMore from Everyday Hacks for Boxed Cake Mix ","summary":"We had a chance to visit Pinnacle Foods this week in Parsippany, New Jersey, to take a look at the test kitchen where Duncan Hines hones their boxed cake mixes and meet the senior research chef in charge there, Joe DiPaolo.  Consumers “want to be inspired,” DiPaolo said.  ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003ESara Bonisteel\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FysR6friq8CAgNrFgV2C7hqHuuLY\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe had a chance to visit&nbsp;Pinnacle Foods&nbsp;this week in Parsippany, New Jersey, to take a look at the test kitchen where&nbsp;Duncan Hines&nbsp;hones their boxed cake mixes and meet the senior research chef in charge there, Joe DiPaolo.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE:&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Farticlesguides\u002Fblogs\u002Feditor\u002F2014\u002F01\u002F10-surprising-foods-you-should-be-keeping-in-your-freezer.html?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E15 Food That Are Better Frozen\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EConsumers “want to be inspired,” DiPaolo said. “I think they want to be able to get more ideas. It’s beyond the cake. It’s what else can you do with it.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMuch of his job is figuring that out. Here are some of the things he’s come up with using the brand’s latest line, Signature Velvet (the various iterations of mixes do matter, because they’re designed to give different kinds of crumb and moisture to a cake).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBars\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&quot;You can take our cake mix, and you can add one egg and a stick of butter and a little bit of water and … make almost a dough out of our cake mix, and then add a cheesecake filling, and put some of our Duncan Hines Comstock [canned fruit] on top of it,&quot; he said. &quot;I’ve actually made it where you take our Devil’s Food cake and our cherry Comstock and now you have a chocolate cherry bar. Or in the summertime do a little peach Comstock with our caramel cake or classic yellow [cake] with blueberry.&quot;\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003ESEE MORE:&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Farticlesguides\u002Fblogs\u002Feditor\u002F2014\u002F02\u002Fchocolate-chip-cookie-baking-tips.html?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E5 Easy Ways to Make Your Chocolate Chip Cookies Even Better\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIce Cream Sandwiches\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003EAdd 1 egg, 1 teaspoon of water, and 2 tablespoons of melted butter to the mix. Combine and spoon rounded teaspoons of the dough about 2 inches apart onto a cookie sheet. Bake at 350 degrees for 10 to 12 minutes to make cookies for the sandwich cookies.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECake Pops\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003EBake cake according to instructions, then crush the cake into crumbs, reserving 1\u002F4 cup of crumbs for garnish. Add 1\u002F2 container of frosting to the crumbed cake, mix, and form into balls. Place on lollipop sticks. Melt the remaining frosting and dip balls into pops. Roll pops in remaining crumbs.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPastry Cream “\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EIf you think of a pastry cream, a pastry cream is a liquid that is thickened by starch,” DiPaolo said. “We have starches in the cake mix. Well I’ve actually made custard from our cake mix. I make the batter [according to the box]. I’m still optimizing it, but you take the batter itself and then you add some milk to it and you cook it on the stove like a pastry cream.” It will make a lot of cream so he bakes one round and uses the rest of the batter for the cream.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EDo you ever doctor up boxed cake mix? the Deal With... Tex-Mex","summary":"When in Texas, eat Tex-Mex. That’s the rule.  ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYou know that thing? That thing that sounds like something you should already know about, so you don’t really want to ask? Well, we know about it, and we’ll give you the intel. Welcome to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ftagged\u002Fwhat&apos;s-the-deal-with\"\u003EWhat’s the Deal With\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FZVOUqFK9ZbKrjUR18LyWl8_O1PU\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: StockFood\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhen in Texas, eat Tex-Mex. That’s the rule. (Oh, and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Ffranklin-bbq-by-the-numbers-78973377166.html\"\u003Ebarbecue\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, of course).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMexican-American, Austin-based chef \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Fchezreneortiz\"\u003ERene Ortiz\u003C\u002Fa\u003E was born in Houston, has lived in Texas for half his life, and has loved Tex-Mex for all of it. The co-founder of Austin’s \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flacondesa.com\u002Faustin\"\u003ELa Condesa\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and New York’s \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fesquinanyc.com\u002F\"\u003ELa Esquina\u003C\u002Fa\u003E knows his way around “regular” \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fbe-a-smarter-diner-at-mexican-restaurants-8-tips-from-77393455962.html\"\u003EMexican\u003C\u002Fa\u003E food, too, which he showcases right alongside Tex-Mex cuisine at the restaurant he currently co-owns,&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ffresaschicken.com\u002F\"\u003EFresa’s Chicken al Carbon\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ETex-Mex is on display in all its glory here at \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fsxsw.com\u002F\"\u003ESXSW\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, so we asked Ortiz to explain to us what exactly it is…including the drippy, lovable \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Frecipes\u002Ffood\u002Fviews\u002FQueso-Fundido-51117480\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003Equeso\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&nbsp;shown here.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhat is Tex-Mex and where did it come from?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ETex-Mex for Texas is the \u003Cem\u003Enorte&ntilde;o\u003C\u002Fem\u003E approach to cooking, or the north-of-Mexico approach, and how Mexicans acclimated in Texas. In the American part of a defining border, how were the Mexicans who were migrating going to acclimate to the changing food? Oftentimes you see [the Mexican influence] in the cheese parts of [Tex-Mex dishes], but sometimes it’s also fun parts like \u003Cem\u003Ebarbacoa \u003C\u002Fem\u003Ein tacos. As you gradually go up from Mexico into Texas, it completely changes. I think Tex-Mex here in Texas is a funny part of so many demographics coming together, trying to work with each other, and fit into one cuisine. A lot of Americans&nbsp;don’t ever get to [experience] that [melding of cultures]. Queso, for example, is the chop suey of Texas.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhat’s its defining characteristic? \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EQueso\u003Cstrong\u003E.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E It is 75% oil and a small part of dairy protein from cheese curds. It’s not healthy, it’s not good for you, but it IS tasty. We sell it at Fresa’s now, and it’s absolutely amazing. We use the white \u003Cem\u003Equeso fresco\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, melt that down with a bit of tomato, garlic, onion, cilantro and jalape&ntilde;os.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EHow did queso come into being?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhat Mexicans brought with them was the skill for the actual making of the cheese. But also, the Native Americans, like my mother, knew how to make cheese. [Mexicans thought], “Hey, let’s add tomatoes, let’s put \u003Cem\u003Epico de gallo\u003C\u002Fem\u003E in there, let’s eat it with \u003Cem\u003Echip\u003C\u002Fem\u003Es!”&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhat are other Tex-Mex staples?&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EA \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.seriouseats.com\u002F2011\u002F06\u002Fpuffy-tacos-san-antonio-texas-diana-barrios.html\"\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003Epuffy taco\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E is one. That’s masa with a little bit more baking soda, and so it puffs in the fryer and looks like \u003Cem\u003Ebembe\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, big lips. You fill them with meat and cheese and they get crunchy, soft, all these textures. They’re delicious because it’s texturally involved. Then there are \u003Cstrong\u003Eenchiladas\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E that are dipped in the sauce, rolled with cheese, and \u003Cem\u003Enot\u003C\u002Fem\u003E fried. You heat them up in salsa, with \u003Cem\u003Ecarne\u003C\u002Fem\u003E on top, and add more cheese on top of that. The\u003Cstrong\u003E combo plate\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E is big; like, a crispy shelled taco, rice, beans, a little bit of guacamole, or the \u003Cstrong\u003Esuper combo plate\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E that comes with a tamale. A tamale in there makes it even better. It’s a hungry man’s dinner, like the American hungry man’s breakfast. \u003Cstrong\u003EQuesadillas\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E, on flour tortillas, always with chicken, cheese, sour cream or guacamole, very simple, always cooked on a griddle.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWill Texas always have Tex-Mex?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIt’ll always be here, since there are a lot of people in Houston that love it, and in San Antonio they love it. But here in Austin it’s a trend that’s slowly starting to dry because as a city it’s very fit.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWill you always have a soft spot for it?&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMi Tierra, one of the oldest Mexican restaurants you can eat at in, with velvet paintings and a beautiful bar. I went there with my brother, and you feel grounded, not only because of your weight—ha!—but you feel grounded in spirit.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYahoo Food is a new site for people who love to eat. :"image\u002Fjpeg","width":100,"height":25,"size_type":"original"}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FFsgAzUaF_j8OBvTbvWKI9gbt2Yw"}],"highlight_color":"#000000","content_status":"UNPUBLISHED","type":"story","title":"How to Make Irish Soda Bread That Isn't Terrible ","summary":"d Irish soda bread is the fruitcake of St. Patrick’s Day.  ","body":"\u003Cdiv\u003E \n \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cem\u003ERecipe by&nbsp;Tori Ritchie, photo by Lisa Hubbard\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002FRG8x6ACczwlmo9RgIifL6bF3zSY\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EIrish soda bread&nbsp;is the fruitcake of St. Patrick’s Day. Every March 17, the bread inspires rumblings of discontent: it’s too dry, it’s cardboard-y, it’s just plain weird-tasting. (And thank goodness we won’t have to eat it for another year.) But really, Irish soda bread can be toothsome and hearty, with a crispy top layer and insides that beg to be slathered with butter. We asked deputy food editor (and Irish girl by marriage) Janet McCracken why soda bread gets a bad rap, and how to make and eat this St. Paddy’s Day staple the right way.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EWhy People Don’t Like It\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThey don’t know what it is.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EIrish soda bread is basically a giant scone–a quick bread. Pick out your favorite scone recipe and compare it to our&nbsp;soda bread recipe. They’ll be almost identical.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThey expect it to be a yeasty bread.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EPeople expect it to taste like a loaf made with yeast, but the only leavener in it is baking soda (hence the name).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIt has caraway seeds in it.\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EWe get it: some people don’t like caraway seeds. Try raisins in the mix, a traditional add-in, and it’s one seriously different flavor combination.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE: \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Fdrinks\u002Farticle\u002Firish-whiskey-recipes?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003ECooking With Irish Whiskey\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIt has buttermilk in it\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E.\u003Cbr\u003ETraditionally, anyway. Most people just use milk, and some crazy cooks even use sour cream. Buttermilk is the way to go, and it gives the bread a subtle tang that is really appealing.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EHow People Should Eat It\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EWhen you’re making your soda bread, make sure you put a big “X” in the very middle of the loaf. The extra surface area will make the top super brown and crispy. Eat it right out of the oven, or slice and toast it the next day and slather it with good Irish butter. Then do it again the next day–this stuff deserves more than its annual due.&nbsp;\u003Cem\u003E–Janet McCracken\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE: \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Frecipes\u002Farticle\u002Fhow-to-cook-with-lucky-charms?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003EYou Can Use Lucky Charms In Your Cooking\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cstrong\u003EBROWN BUTTER SODA BREAD\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \n \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cbr\u003ERosemary and black pepper make this bread anything but typical. Wedges are delicious with plenty of butter and your favorite preserves.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E \n \u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EIngredients:\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E1\u002F4 cup (1\u002F2 stick) unsalted butter \n \u003Cbr\u003E3&nbsp;1\u002F2 cups all purpose flour \n \u003Cbr\u003E1\u002F2 cup old-fashioned oats \n \u003Cbr\u003E1 tablespoon sugar \n \u003Cbr\u003E1 tablespoon chopped fresh rosemary \n \u003Cbr\u003E2 teaspoons baking powder \n \u003Cbr\u003E1 teaspoon baking soda \n \u003Cbr\u003E1 teaspoon salt \n \u003Cbr\u003E3\u002F4 teaspoon ground black pepper plus additional for topping \n \u003Cbr\u003E1&nbsp;3\u002F4 cups buttermilk \n \u003Cbr\u003E1 egg white, beaten to blend \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EPreparation:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003EPosition rack in center of oven and preheat to 375&deg;F. Stir butter in heavy small saucepan over medium heat until melted and golden brown, about 3 minutes. Remove from heat.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EStir flour, oats, sugar, rosemary, baking powder, baking soda, salt, and 3\u002F4 teaspoon pepper in large bowl to blend. Pour buttermilk and melted browned butter over flour mixture; stir with fork until flour mixture is moistened.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESEE MORE:&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bonappetit.com\u002Fentertaining-style\u002Fholidays\u002Farticle\u002Fcanned-corned-beef-for-st-patrick-s-day?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003ECanned Corned Beef Taste-Test\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003ETurn dough out onto floured work surface. Knead gently until dough comes together, about 7 turns. Divide in half. Shape each half into ball; flatten each into 6-inch round. Place rounds on ungreased baking sheet, spacing 5 inches apart. Brush tops with beaten egg white. Sprinkle lightly with ground black pepper. Using small sharp knife, cut 1\u002F2-inch-deep X in top of each dough round.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EBake breads until deep golden brown and tester inserted into center comes out clean, about 45 minutes. Cool breads on rack at least 30 minutes. Serve warm or at room temperature.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EBaker’s Wisdom:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E You’ll get the most tender soda bread by kneading the dough gently and briefly, just until it comes together, so the gluten is minimally developed.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESee more from Bon Yes—Black-Eyed Pea Vodka Is Possible","summary":"It’s a funny thing, vodka—nearly anything with starch or sugar can be transformed into the clear, fiery spirit.  There’s milk vodka, fig vodka, cactus vodka, and now: black-eyed pea vodka. ","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs1.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002Foriginal\u002F21xs_yf6ADWZ5JM8qFiHjHTVSPc\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPhoto credit: Getty\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIt’s a funny thing, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fbooze-map-of-the-world-crushes-our-spirits-74417073147.html\"\u003Evodka\u003C\u002Fa\u003E—nearly anything with starch or sugar can be transformed into the clear, fiery spirit. There’s \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fnewsfeed.time.com\u002F2013\u002F04\u002F12\u002Fbritish-dairy-farmer-creates-pure-milk-vodka\u002F\"\u003Emilk vodka\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.figenza.com\u002F\"\u003Efig vodka\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.spikevodka.com\u002Fabout-spike.html\"\u003Ecactus vodka\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and now: black-eyed pea vodka.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&quot;It’s never been done before, that we can find,&quot; said Deborah Nickels, who, with her grown son Trey, is the force behind&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Funhingedproductionsllc.com\u002F\"\u003ETreyMark Black-Eyed Vodka\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. By mid-May, the pair hopes to begin production in a historic firehouse south of downtown Fort Worth, Texas. Bottles will \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.star-telegram.com\u002F2014\u002F03\u002F03\u002F5618147\u002Fnew-fort-worth-vodka-gives-peas.html\"\u003Esell for around $35\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, similar to other top-shelf vodkas.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&quot;As far as the taste, it’s very smooth with no burn,&quot; Nickels told us in a lush Texan drawl. &quot;Our vodka comes off with a little nut finish at the end.&nbsp;Also some people have said it has a subtle green tea finish.&quot; It’s delightful in a dirty martini or spicy \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ffood\u002Fits-gettin-hot-in-here-71340443543.html\"\u003EBloody Mary\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, she said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut nearly as intriguing as the vodka itself is the story behind it. For decades, Nickels and her family tended fields upon fields of black-eyed peas across&nbsp;three West Texas counties, producing beans for large outfits like&nbsp;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.bushbeans.com\u002Fen_US\u002Findex.jsp\"\u003EBush Brothers &amp; Company\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIt was hard yet rewarding work, Nickels explained. Sometimes Trey, exhausted from driving up to 350 miles a day between fields, would sleep with his crops rather than driving home through the night.&nbsp;But a series of droughts and shrinking water supplies left the family flailing, uncertain what to do next.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ELate one evening, drinking beer in a black-eyed pea field, Trey had an idea: Why not make vodka? The Nickels’ enlisted the help of a distilling consultant, Kentuckian \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.artisanresources.com\u002F\"\u003ESherman Owens\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, who developed a way to break down the legumes based on a Chinese method of extracting starch from mung beans.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe elder Nickels is certainly proud of her son’s savvy. “Trey gave me a chance to come along on his dream,” she said, her voice welling. “It’s really a story of a son giving his mom a new chance. At my age, it’s not an easy thing to come by.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe wish the Nickels lots of luck.
